Effective\n\nReduction\n\nModule.\n\nStudies.\nStudy\n\n133\nResults.\n\nXlll\n\n135\n\n1.0\n\nSUHHARY\n\nA program\nwas initiated\nwith the General\nElectric\nCompany to conduct\nperformance deterioration\nstudies\nfor the CF6-50 high bypaso\nturbofan\nengine.\nThe\nbasic\nobjectives\nwere to determine\nthe specific\ncauses\nof deterioration\nwhich\nresult in increased fuel consumption (performance loss), and to identify potential ways for minimizing or eliminating these effects.\nNormal flight recordinks and test cell performance data were ar.aly.-.edt well as data from hardas\nware inspect ions at airline- overhaul shops, in order to define the extent\nand magnitude ,)fengin,, deterioration.\nThese stodies\nindicated\nthat the rates of deterioration\ndiffered\nmarkedly\nb\xc2\xb0tween that of operation\nduring\naircraft\ncheckout procedures\n(short\nterm)\nand revenue _ervice\n(long term).\nThe short-term\nlosses occurred rapi\'ly,\nwhereas the long-term\nlosses exhibited\na gradual\nrate,\nThe level\nof shortterm losses was determined\nto be equivalent\nto 0.7 percent\nin cruise\nfuel\nburn (cruis_\n0fc)\nfor all three\naircraft\ntypes (OC-lO-30,\nA3OO-B, and Is767)\ncurrently\npowered by CF6-50 engines.\nAn assessment\nof performance\ndeterioration\nwith respect\nto individual\nmodules was not possible\nfor the shirtterm aspect,\nsince\nhardware\ninspections\nare not conducted\nfollowing\nthe\ncheckout flights.\nBecause operational\nconditions\nvary,\nand since the airlines\npractice\nan "oncondition"\nengine maintenance\nconcept,\nthe average period\nof revenue service\nfor an engine\'s\ninitial\ninstallation\nvaries\nsomewhat\nwith respect\nto individual aircraft\ntype.\nThe average\nlong-term\nperformance\ndeterioration\noccurring\nfrom the time of an engine\'s\nfirst\nrevenue\nservice\nflight\nuntil\nit is returned\nto a m:lint,,nan(\'+,\nfaci!itv\ntt,r domonst:-:,*d.\n\niapact\n\ntoward\n\nenergy\n\nconservat\n\nion\n\nir\n\nthe\n\ni\n\n!\n\n2.0\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nA program was initiated for the CF6 family of turbofan engines to identify and\nquantify the causes of performance deterioration which increase fuel consumption. The recent energy demand has outpaced domestic fuel supplies creating\nan increased United States dependence on foreign oil. This increased dependence was accentuated by the OPEC embargo in the winter of 1973-1974 which\ntriggered a rapid rise in the price of fuel. This price rise, along with the\npotential for further increases, brought about a set of changing economic circumstances _Lth regard to the use of energy.\nThese events were felt in all\nsectors of the transportation industry. As a result, the C_vernment with the\nsupport o_ the aviation industry, initiated programs\naimed et both the supply\nand demand aspects of the problem.\nThe supply aspect is being investigated\nby determining the fuel availability from new sources such as coal and oil\nshale, with concurrent programs in progress to develop engine combustors and\nfuel systems to accept these broader based fuels.\nReduced fuel consumption is the approach being employed to deal with the demand aspect of the problem.\nAccordingly, NASA is sponsoring the Aircraft\nEnergy Efficiency (ACEE) program which is directed toward reducing fuel consumptLon for commercial air transports.\nThe long-range effort to reduce fuel\nconsumption is expected to evolve new technology which will permit development\nof a more energy efficient turbofan, or an improved propulsion cycle such as\nthat for turboprops.\nStudies have indicated large reductions in fuel usage\nare possible (e.g., 15 to 40 percent); from this approach, however, a significant impact in fuel usage is considered to be 15 or more years away. In the\nnear term, the only practical propulsion approach is to improve the fuel efficiency of current engines since these engines will continue to be the significant fuel users for the next 15 to 20 years.\nThe Engine Component Improvement (ECI) program is the element of the ACEE program directed at improving the fuel efficiency of current engines.\nThe ECI\nprogram consists of two parts:\n(I) Performance Improvement and (2) Engine\nDiagnostics.\nThe Performance Improvement program is directed at developing\nengine\nperformance\nimprovement\nand retention\nconcepts\nfor new production\nand\nretrofit engines,\nthe Engine Diagnostics effort is to provide information\nrelated to determining the sources and magnitudes of performance deterioration\nfor the high bypass ratio turbofan eng{nes utilized on wide-body aircraft.\n\n*\n\nAs part of the Engine Diagnostics effort, NASA-Lewis initisted a progrm with\nthe General\nElectric\nCompany to conduct\nperfo.-mance\ndeterioration\nstudies\nfor\nthe CF6-6D and CF6-50 model engine.\nThe basic\nobjectives\nof the program were\n(I) to determine the specific causes for engine deterioration which increase\nfuel burn, (2) to isolate short-term losses from the longer term tosses snd,\n(3) to identify potential ways to minimize the deterioration effects.\nThis\nreport covers the investigation of the CF6-50 engine model; a prior report\n(Reference l) summarizes the findings for the CF6-6D model engine.\n\n3\n\nI\n\n3.0\n\nAPPROACII\n\nFigure\n3-I\nprovides\na graphical\nrepresentation\nof iorat\n\nion\n\nInitial InstallationThe performance\ndeterioration\ncharacteristics\nfor the\nin{tiM\ninstaltatlon\nof the engine\nis of primary\nconcern\nto the engine\nmanufacturer,\nsince\nit is only during\nthis\nperiod\nthat\nan engine\nis tested\nwith\nall new components.\nAs such, the deterioration characteristics provide considerable insight into the durability capabilities of the engine, which identifies any need or needs\nfor specific\nprtxtuct\nimprovements.\nOn the other\nhand,\nsince the initial installation engine\nrepresents only a small\npercentage\nof\nthe entire\nfleet,\nthese\ndata\nare less\nmeaningful\nto the airlines.\nTwo general\ntypes\nof performance\ndata\nwere utilized\nto determine\nthe long-term\ndeterioration\ncharacteristics\nfor the production\nnew engine.\nFirst\ncockpit\ncruise\nrecordings\nobtainad\nby the airlines\nduring\nrevenue\nservice\nwere accumulated\nand analyzed\nto produce\ndeterioration\ntrends.\nThese cruise\ndata\nwhich\nwere generally obtained from in-house General Electric files are in various\nformats, but all are expressed in delta deviations.\nThe baseline in all cases\nis the applicable aircraft manufacturer\'s flight manual which includes expected\nengine parameters for various flight conditions.\nThe second source of available performance data was from a limited number of test cell recallbrations of\ndeteriorated engines prior to refurbishment.\n\nCruise Trend Data:\nThere was a large quantity of revenue service cockpit recordings from which\nto statistically\nderive\ntl_e magnitude\nand trends\nof cruise\nperformance\ndeterioration.\nThe airlines\ncurrently\nuse cruise\ndata\nto derive\nmonthly\ntrends\nfor\nthe average\nof all\nengines\nin their\nfleets.\nNhile\nthis\nprocedure\nproduces\na\nstatistical\nfuel burn\ntrend\nfor the fleet\n(information\nof major\neconomic\nconcern),\nit provides\nvery little\ninsight\ninto the deterioration\ncharacteristics\nof the individual\nengines.\nTherefore,\nfor the benefit\nof this\nprogram,\nthe\nrevenue\nservice\ncruise\ndata\nwere analyzed\nby tracking\nthe performance\nlevel\nfrom instal\nt_t ion to removal\nfor specific\nindividual\nengines\non a regular\nbasis\n(monthly,\nor _s regularly\nas feasible).\nThis method not only permitted\nassessment of engine\nperformance\ndeterioration\ncharacteristics\nas required\nto satisfy\nprogram\nobjectives,\nbut also\nproduced\nthe necessary\ncomparative\ndata\nrequired\nto identify\npotential\neffects\nfrom operational\nvariables.\nBecause\neach engine\nwas being trended on the same basis of A cruise sfc since installation (baseline earliest cruise data available), it was possible to group the individual\ntrends\nto derive\na statistical\naverage\nby adjusting\nthe initial\nstarting\npoints\nto a zero or common reference.\nThe airlines\ntrend\nfuel\nflow and other\ncruise\nperformance\nparameters\nat constant\nfan speed,\nnot thrust.\n1"his provides\na convenient\nand effective\nmethod\nfor trending\nperformance\nchanges\nfor conditian\nmonitoring\npurposes,\nltowever,\nthe actual\nfuel burn increase\n(cruise\nsfc)\ncaused\nby deterioration\nis different\nthan actually\nmeasured\nby changes\nin fuel\nflow at fan speed based on\ncycle\ndeck and other\nanalytical\nmodels\nused to compare\nsea level\nwith cruise\noperation.\nTherefore,\nit is necessary\nto calculate\nthese\nchanges\nin cruise\n\n11\n\nsfc,\nsured\nof a\ncruise\ndelta\nwere\n\nsince\ninstalled\nthrust,\nrequired\nto calculate\ncruise\nsfc,\ncannot\nbe meain flight.\nBased on previous\nexperiences\nusing the mathematical\nmodel\nthermodynamic cycle\nfor a deteriorated\nCF6-50 engine,\nthe change in\nsfc is equivalent\nto approxiuately\n0.9 percent of the cruise\nfuel flow\nat constant\nfan speed.\nThe cruise\nsfc levels\ndiscussed\nin this\nreport\nderived\nusing this\nfactor.\n\nCruise\nperformance\ntrend data were collected\nand analyzed\nfor the initial\ninstallation\nof CFb-50 engiues\ninstalled\non the three different\naircraft.\nStatistical\ncurve\nfits\nof these\ndata were studied\nusing\nleast-square\npolynomial\ncurve\nfit\ntechniques\nfor polynomials\nto the third\ndegree\nof the form:\n\n\xc2\xa5 = AO\n\n+ A3X3\n\nStepwise\nregression\ntechniques\nwere used to identlfy\nthe "best\nfit"\ncurves\nfor the normalized\ndata,\nand the resultant\nfits\nfor all three\naircraft\ntypes\nwas linear\nfor both hours\nand cycles.\nThere\nis a wide spread\nin the individual\ndata points;\nhowever,\nthe confidence\nlevels\nassociated\nwith the average\ntrends\nare over 99 percent.\nThe fuel\nflow and EGT high\npressure\ncompressor\n(HPC) can result\nfrom a number of factors.\nThese factors\ncan be generally\nclassified\nas either\nairfoil\ntip clearance\nchanges,\nairfoil\nquality\ndegradation,\nor vane-to-rotor\nspool\nclearances\ncan result\nfrom\ncoating\nlosses\nor rubs,\nand from blades\nand vanes\nwhich may have been shortened in prior\nrubs or as a result\nof inadequate\nshop procedures.\nFlowpath\nCoatings\nused in the HPC are intended\nto provide\nprotection\nfrom\nabrasion\nof the casing\nand rotor\nstructures\nto provide\nan abradable\nmaterial\nwhich allows\nfor small\nlocal\nrubs without\ncausing\na major change\nin blade or\nvane height,\nand to minimise\nor prevent\nlIPC mechanical\ndamage.\nThe abrasive\nmaterial\nused is Metro 450 plasma\nspray,\nwhich\naffords\nprotection\nfor the\ncasing\nand rotor\nstructural\nmembers.\nThis material\nalso\nprovides\na bonding\nsurface\nfor an abradable\naluminum spray coating\nused in the forward\nstages\nas shown in Figure\n4-45.\nRevenue\nservice\nexperience\nindicated,\nhowever,\nthat\nthe aft\nstages\nof aluminum\ncoating\nsuffered\ndegradation\ndue to spalling.\nThis\ncondition\noccurs\nas a result\nof thermal\ncycling\nwhich causes\nthe aluminum/\nMetro 450 bond coat\ninterface\nto fail\ndue to the differences\nin thermal\nexpansion\nbetween\nthe aluminum\na typical\nexample\nof spalled\n\nand structural\ncoatings.\n\nsurface.\n\nFigure\n\n4-46\n\nillustrates\n\nFigure\n4-47 shows the percentage\nof coating\nspalled\nfor the aft stages\nof\nthe compressor\nafter\n4,000\nand 8,000\nhours.\nEvidence\nof spalled\ncoatings\non\nthe rotor\nforward\nof Stage\n7, and on the casing\nforward\nof Stage\n8 was nonexistent.\nThe average\nlosses\nin the aluminum\ncoating\nresult\nin a clearance\nincrease\nof approximately\n0.015\ninch,\nand small\nadditional\nsfc\nlosses\ndue to\nincreases\nin flowpath\nsurface\nroughness.\n\n"\n\nAirfoil\nTip Rubs produce\nincreased\nradial\ntip clearances,\nincreased\nroughness\nof the rubcoat,\nand roughness\nof the airfoil\nsurface\nfinish\ndue to the deposited\naluminum\ndebris\nliberated\nas a result\nof the rub.\nTip rubs are most\nlikely\nto occur during\ntakeoff\nat aircraft\nrotation,\nor during\na hot rotor\nreburst\nat low altitudes.\nRotor blade\nrubs were observed\non the aluminum\ncoating\nof the upper casing\nin Stages\n3 through\n9 over an arc of abou\n120\ndegrees and to a maximum depth of about O.OlO inch. The performance effects\nfrom these rubs are small, since (unlike in the lipturbine) the rubs locally\nremove the coating material with only a negligible change in blade length.\nVane tip-to-spool Pressure\nTurbine\nSection\n- The primary\ncauses\nof high pressure\nturbine\n(HPT) deterioration\nare changes\nin blade\ntip-to-shroud\nclearance,\nalrfoit\nsurface finish,\nand rub\nresulting\nin a significant\nincrease\nin average\nclearance.\nThis has led to\nthe development\nof a procedure\nfor monitoring\nblade\ntip rubs without\nengine\ndisassembly.\nNotches\n_f realized\nby more effective\nand/or additional\nrestoration\nduring each shop visit.\nWhile reducing\nenergy consumption\nis important,\nthe\nairlines\nrequire\nthat any maintenance\nover and above that required\nto restore\nmechanical\ncapabilit\nies be cost-effect\nire.\nThe\ni\n\nGeneral\n\nElectric\n\nhardware\n\nteams\n\nconducted\n\ncost-effectiveness\n\nfeasl-\n\nbility\nstudies\nfor each of the deterioration\nitems.\nThese\nstudies\nconsisted\nsimply of calculating\nthe labor\nand material\ncosts,\n_ith\nno attempt\nto incorporate\nother\nfinancial\nconsiderations.\nNote:\nthe individua,\nairline_ \xc2\xb0 have\ntheir\nown distinct\nground rules\non what is to be included\nin cost\neffectiveness studies,\nand generally\ninclude:\nlabor and fuel\ncosts,\nburden (overhead)_\ninvestment\nand cost\nof money.\nThe studies\nwere not directed\ntoward any specific\nairline(s),\nas the required\ninput data are considered\nproprietary\nand,\nhence,\ndirect\nairline\ninput was not available.\nReasonable\nassumptions\nwere\nused by the teams based on normal\nairline\nrelationships\nto determine\nthe criteria\nto use for these\nstudies.\nIt is for these\nreasons\nthat\nthese\nstudies\nare termed\nadjust\nthe\n\ncost-effectiveness\nresults\nto its\n\nfeaslbi1:ty\nown specific\n\nstudies,\ncriteria.\n\nand\n\neach\n\nairline\n\nhas\n\nto\n\nSTUDY CRITERIA\nCost-effectiveness\nstudies\nconsist\nof two major\nitems:\nadditional\ncosts\nfor\nthe modification,\nand expected\nreduction\nin operation\ncosts,\n- the difference\nrepresents\nthe potential\nsavings.\nAdditional\ncosts\nwere calculated\nt_sing a\nlabor rate of $36.00/hour\nt,which includes\nan estimated\noral\'head\nof 200 percent)\nand material\ncosts\nbased on the General\nElectric\n1979 parts\ncatalog.\nThe teas\nestimated\nthe labor needed\nto perform\nthe modifications\nand included\nthe man-hours\nrequired\nto disassemble/reinstall\nthe individual\nparts\ninto the\nspecific\nmodules.\nIt was assumed\nthat\nthe individual\nmodules\nwere exposed\nas\na result\nof normal mechanical\nrepair.\nThe "cost\nof money" was not _ncluded\nin the study,\nnor were any investment\ncriteria\n("bricks\nand mortar").\nThe\ndetermination\nof material\ncosts\nassumed\nthe vendor\nrepair\ncost\nrather\nthan\nneb--par _. cost,\nwhere applicable.\n\n.\n;\n\nThe calculation\nof reduced\noperating\nmount\nof performance\nto be until\n\nrepresents\n\nthe\n\nrate\n\nof the\nthe\nat\n\nwhich the gain deteriorates.\nThe useful\nlife\nand life\nfactor\nwere derived\nfor each condition\nby the hardware\nteams based on their\nobservations,\nother\nGeneral\nElectric\nstudies\nor tests,\nanalytical\ncalculations\nand common sense.\nFigure\n5-I schematically\ndescribes\nthe life\nfactor\nand useful\nlife\nitems.\nTwo
